1. How to forget something quickly?
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 06 Jan 2005 18:35:41 -0000

Hey everyone, I've been practicing BLD solving a lot lately and I had a question I wanted to bring up. I know most of blindfolded solving is about minimizing memory interference, so that during the solve you don't forget any of what you memorized and mess up the solve. However, I wanted to ask what is the best way to forget something quickly? Whenever I practice BLD solving, I do several solves in a row. I do this until I either have to stop to leave and go do something else (since BLD solving takes a while), or until I have so many bits and pieces of cycles and piece orientations in my head that memorizing a new cycle takes a whole lot of effort. I've tried doing several normal sighted solves in between blindfold solves to try to allow myself to forget the cycles I had previously. It seems to work alright, but to be honest I haven't tried it a lot yet. I guess my question is, are there any useful ways to quickly forget a lot of stuff you have just memorized? Is there a way to use interference (doing something else for a while) to make sure you forget the cycles and orientations of the cube you just did? It always seems that my best BLD times come from either the first or second solve I do, since my head is still clear. Would meditation help to do this? I'd like to have that clear mind ease of memorizing for every solve instead of just the first two. Chris
2. Re: How to forget something quickly?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 06 Jan 2005 18:44:36 -0000

I'd simply use a different memo route each time ;-) Now I always put two items at one position which saves space (I need only 10-15 positions), adds more associations (which is good for remembering) and is faster. A suggestion that might help for your way: Between two 3x3 BLD solves, do a pyraminx BLD solve or a skewb BLD solve or whatever. This might "overwrite" your memory a bit because it's "similar" data, but there's almost no way to get confused because the shapes of the puzzles are so different. Cheers! 4. Re: How to forget something quickly?
From: "mackymakisumi" <mackymakisumi@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 08 Jan 2005 02:52:36 -0000

I've only had this problem a few times. One time was when I tried to do a 3x3x3 after doing a 4x4x4. I'm guessing that, because I tend to rush the memorization on 3x3x3, anything I memorize usually doesn't stick for very long. Regarding order...I memorize CP EP EO CO and solve CO EO CP/EP. If I can't remember CP at first or if I feel that I'm forgetting EP, I solve EP and then CP. I don't have any trouble memorizing EP right after CP, probably because I memorize CP with images while I use numbers for EP. 6. Number of...
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 13 Jan 2005 01:30:28 -0000

Concerning the rules for: Rubik's Cube 3x3x3: Blindfold (number of cubes solved) They say when I attempt six cubes and solve five of them, I can't claim to have solved five cubes. I think this is bad. Actually I've probably solved 5.9 cubes, e.g. when the mistake is only two flipped edges or some 3-cycle. I think it should count as 5 cubes. Both speedcubing and memorizing allow mistakes. In Speedcubing, best and worst times are eliminated before we take the average and moreover, we allow an extra solve for a pop. In memorizing, for example memorizing many digits in an hour, I think the lines of digits count separately. If you make one mistake in a row, you only get half of the points for it (e.g. 20 instead of 40). If you make two mistakes, you get zero. But only for that line. All other lines still count. I wonder where that rule for blindsolving the cube came from. In my view, neither does it make sense nor is it consistent with speedcubing/memorizing procedures. What do you think? Stefan

10. New way to orient
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 13 Jan 2005 19:52:12 -0000

I had this idea after Dror popped an edge in Amsterdam, found it but unfortunately inserted it the wrong way. Had he prepared for this case, he could've inserted it the right way by exploiting that inside the cubie you can *feel* the orientation. This could of course be used in general, too. For orienting the pieces, you could take them out one after the other and put them back oriented correctly. If you resticker your cube so that it's a simple structure (e.g. the hole inside the corners is always opposite the white/yellow sticker) then you don't even have to learn much. Cheers! Stefan

16. About the Stackmat 2
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 17 Jan 2005 21:55:28 -0000

Hey guys, I don't know if a lot of people have gotten the Stackmat 2 yet, but for those who haven't yet I've found a slight annoyance about it for blindfolded solving. If you look at the pictures of the base timer part, you'll see that the reset button is very close to the right hand pad. This makes it possible to both stop the timer and hit the reset button at the same time when solving blindfolded. I've done this a few times getting used to the timer already. It's not so bad for speed solving since you can see where to put your hands, but if you're not careful at the end of a BLD solve you'll lose your time before you even get to see it. Just a heads up, Chris

18. Re: I want all algorithms you used for blindfoldsolving.Thank you!
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 20 Jan 2005 21:23:03 -0000

Hello, Here are most of the core important algs I use when blindsolving. I use all of these algs, with setup moves, for other longer algs as well to be able to handle lots of different situations effectively. Corner orientation: 2 corners at once: 1) LU2L'U'LU'L'R'U2RUR'UR 2) the inverse of the above is also useful R'U'RU'R'U2RLUL'ULU2L' 4 corners at once 1) L'U2LUL'U'LUL'UL2U'LULULU'L'U'L2 Edge orientation: 2 edges at once 1) MUMUMU2M'UM'UM'U2 4 edges at once 1) (M'U)X4 2) R2 D' r2 (M'U)x4 r2 D R2 6 edges at once 1) (RBR'U)X5 8 edges at once 1) (r R d D)x3 2) M2 d (r R d D)x2 r R D M2 superflip 1) [ (M' U)x4 y x' ]x3 Corner/edge permutation: See the algorithms page at www.speedcubing.com I would also strongly recommend learning all the algorithms that swap only a single pair of corners and a single pair of edges. You can also use setup moves to use one of the algorithms above to flip different groups of edges. For example to flip the middle layer edges I would do F' L F (E L)x4 F' L' F You can recognize the (M' U)x4 in the form of (E L)x4 (it is only done from a different view). Also you can see that I just do a 3 move setup algorithm at the beginning, and then undo it at the end. I would like to soon put up a more extensive list of the more common algorithms I use (with setup moves included) on my website. Those algs listed above are pretty much all of the core algorithms I use though for solving blindfolded. Chris --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "guotiancong" <guotiancong@y...> wrote: > > > > I am a beginer of blindfoldsolving. > I want to get algorithms as more as possible to improve... > Could you list the algorithms you used for blindfoldsolving or give > me some advice to find some algorithms. > > Thanks a lot.

20. 6 edge flip method
From: smoothcuber <no_reply@yahoogroups.com>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 23 Jan 2005 16:29:47 -0000

Hey, I like using the move (UR'FR)*5; I use it to solve all the 6 edge flip cases. Of course, this requires setup moves. I found the setup moves often difficult to quickly come up with, and I got bored with doing 6 edges as 4, then 2, so I made up a little system for myself. Here it is: Step 1: Get each layer's (U,E(horizontal middle slice),D) unoriented edges into a "start" position by turning U,E, and D as needed. Step 2: Use a short setup move to get pieces into position to apply (UR'FR)*5. Step 3: Do (UR'FR)*5. Step 4: Undo the setup moves from 1 and 2. First, designate a "start" position for each layer's unoriented edges. For example, if there are 2 unoriented, opposing edges on U, they could "start" at UR-UL, or UF-UB, you choose. 3 unoriented on U could start at UF-UB-UR, or UF-UB-UL, etc. 2 unoriented, adjacent D edges could start at DF-DR, or DF-DL, etc. So, choose a start position for each case, each layer. Second, step 2, learn the setup moves for each case. After you get the edges to the start position, you still need to get them into position to apply (UR'FR)*5, so use a short setup move. Here's an example: Unoriented edges are at UR, UL, FL, FR, BR, and DL. Step 1: My start position for 2 opposing edges on U is UF-UB, so I would need to do the move U'(or U) to get the U layer to it's start position. My start position for 3 edges on the E layer is FL-FR-BR, so no move is needed for the E layer here. And for 1 edge on D, I start at DF, so I need to do the move D here. So the full step 1 setup is U'D. Step 2: For this case my setup move is R U2 R2. That gets the edges into position. Step 3: Do (UR'FR)*5. Step 4: Undo the setup moves from 1 and 2: R2 U2 R' D' U. That's it. I hope it makes sense. It's harder to explain than to do. After a while, it gets pretty easy to see how to move U,E,D to the start positions, and the step 2 setup moves are not bad to learn, many are 1 or 2 turns. So not as much to learn as it may seem reading this. The fun part is doing the 6 edge cases in "one" move. Anyhow, maybe this will be of use to someone, hope so. -Dave

27. Skewb blindfolded
From: Kåre Krig <karkr936@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 10 Feb 2005 14:56:25 +0100

I just cut a lot of time from my record for blindfolded skewb. But I need some input about how to define lucky cases for this puzzle. My method works as folows: 1. One group of four corners are permuted by default. 2. For other four corners there are three permutation cases. I All four correct. Lucky I guess. II Two pairs need to be solved. I do this with an easy 10-move alg. But after corner orientations. III 3-cycle of corners. This is done by a single twist, the orientations and center permutations are memorized as they will be after this twist. 3. Orientations, twist one corners clockwise and one counter clockwise, or three the same way. Usually have to do more than one alg here. 4. Center permutation. Currently I only know the 3-cycles, so other cases have to be done in many steps. My record solve was like this. 3-cycle of corners, this is the most likely case, so not lucky at all. One alg to twist two corners, and then another to twist three more. Not lucky bot not the worst possible. Finaly centers was just a 3-cycle, and this is the part I'm not sure about. The time for this solve was 1:35.72. My previous record was 2:18.58 with a 5-cycle of centers. /Kåre
28. Re: Skewb blindfolded
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 10 Feb 2005 16:38:13 -0000

Hi Kare, first of all - congratulations! I certainly need to improve a lot to catch you :-) My method is almost the same as yours, though I stupidly think/memorize two twists ahead in case 2II where I should really use an alg like you do instead. A quick math shot now... I think there are 360 (=6!/2) possible permutations and 40 (=6C3*2!) of them are 3-cycles. That's a 1/9 chance which doesn't sound too bad. For the 3x3 if three corners are solved and the rest are one 5-cycle that's 1/30 and this is defined as non-lucky (it's 8!=40320 possible permutations and 8C5*4!=1344 are 5-cycles). Maybe that's not the best comparison because for the skewb centers there's nothing between 3 correct and 6 correct, whereas there are many more possibilities for the 3x3 corners. What's the probability for exactly five corners out of place? So I'd guess it's of course a little lucky but not lucky enough to discard it as "lucky case". Cheers! 35. Re: If I could only...
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 17 Feb 2005 13:48:34 -0000

Sorry for the delay, but I wanted to wait until it's included in the speedcubingdotcom list. I didn't want to influence the decision at all of how "amazing" to rate it. Note that "amazing" is not proportional to "difficult". The super 5x5 is *easier* than the normal 5x5, though I agree for most people out there it's more "amazing". Same for (super) 4x4, so you still have to prove you can solve a 4x4 blindfolded ;-) For me personally, nothing in that list is "amazing" anymore. I can still be impressed but not really amazed. If that sounds arrogant, I'm sorry. It's not. Compare it to any non-cuber who watches you solve the cube (just normal, not blindfolded) and calls that "amazing". For him/her it might be, but for us? That's because we know some things about it, good systems and strategies. And once you know a bit about blindsolving and memorization techniques and what memorizers are capable of, you simply can't be amazed much anymore. Ok, back to your question and why the super version is easier. I'd say blindsolving consists of three parts: - Analysis (find out what you have to do) - Memorization - Solving I memorize super 5x5 and normal 5x5 exactly the same way. I solve them the same way (well, parities and center orientations are slightly different, but that's nothing). But analysis! Analyzing the super 5x5 is much easier, cause this sweetie is nice enough to exactly tell you where each piece belongs. So you can just "read" what you have to do. The normal 5x5 forces you to *decide* where the equivalent pieces shall go. That's a complete extra task and a pretty ugly one. Cheers! Stefan --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > Stefan, that's awesome - congratulations! Did you find it very > difficult to remember all the pieces, or was it pretty much the same > as a normal blindfolded 5x5 solve (except maybe the possibility of the > center cycles being a bit different)? > > That's amazing! > > Chris > > --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" > <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > > > ... motivate myself more often... > > http://www.stefan-pochmann.de/spocc/blindsolving/super5x5/

Hi Stephan, I do think I know what went wrong with the three-cycle. On one solve I had a situation with the X centers that required two pair swaps. One of the pair swaps was 15 - 8. What I actually did by mistake was 16 - 8. I can't explain why I made such a simple error but it did result in the entire cube being supersolved except for a three cycle of X centers 8 - 16 - 15. To make matters even more heartbreaking in the numbering system I use 8 is on the Back face and 15 16 are on the Left face. Thus when I took my blindfold off it looked like the cube was solved, my excitement started to rise, and then I looked to the left only to be crushed by a simple little error. Ironically it is exactly for this reason that I find blindsolving so appealing - there is NO ROOM FOR ERROR and it is so ruthlessly unforgiving - you either get it or you don't - there is no in between. Let's definately compare notes after I nail the solve which should be in the near future. 40. First-time solve.
From: "Ben Marx" <godsalgorithm@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 20 Feb 2005 06:36:31 -0000

I know this stuff is old-hat to a lot of you by now, but after solving a few cubes without looking at the cube - but with notes, I just attempted to do a real solve and succeeded. It took 31 minutes to memorize and 17 minutes to solve. I know that's slow, but it'll be fun to try to get that time down to a quicker speed like a lot of you guys. I'd like to thank Stefan Pochmann for helping me figure out a blindfold method which works well for me. I still use the orient corners and orient edges steps separately, like on Grant's site. But using the idea of placing one cubie at a time with "buffer zones" (with a few personal modifications)from Stephan's site, is what enabled me to do it.

It's easy to wear while memorizing and just slip over your eyes when you're ready to start solving. Also when you fold one that many times (to make such a small band) you can't see through it at all. 48. Some math about competitive BLD solving
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 04 Apr 2005 16:41:55 -0000

Hey everyone, I recently looked at some of the probability related to blindfold solving in competitions. ------------------- Here is how I looked at blindfold solving. I started with three assumptions: 1) The faster you try to go, the worse your chances for getting a successful solve. 2) In a competition your goal is only to get the fastest solve you possibly can. 3) You absolutely under no circumstances want to DNF every solve you are given in a round. This forces your elimination which makes #2 impossible. Starting with these assumptions I looked at the odds of making sure you get at the very least one successful solve out of either the 2 or 3 solves given. --------------------------- Given 2 solves in the round --------------------------- Call your personal success rate in practice the variable x. Now the odds of you failing a solve are (1-x), and the odds of you failing both solves given are (1-x)^2. So the odds of successfully completing at least one solve in a competition round given two solves is 1-(1-x)^2 To satisfy #3 above, the urge to not be eliminated in a tournament, I said that the odds of getting at least one successful solve in a competition should at the very worst be 99% So now we have to solve 0.99=1-(1-x)^2 0=0.01-(1-x)^2 and by factoring the difference of squares we get, 0=0.01+1-x or 0=0.01-1+x x=1.1 or x=0.9 And since these numbers are probabilities we must choose x=0.9 So in order to nearly guarantee that you are not eliminated in a tournament given 2 solves you have to solve with at worst 90% accuracy. I call the 90% solving "break neck" speed, since it is the fastest you can go and still be safe in knowing you will almost certainly not be eliminated. --------------------------- Given 3 solves in the round --------------------------- By the same logic I'm trying to find an equation to show my odds of getting at least one successful solve in a competition round with 3 solves, given that I have success rate x in practice. My chances of failure are (1-x) and my chances of failing all three solves is (1-x)^3. So the chances of getting at least one successful solve is 1-(1-x)^3 Again, we want this chance at the very worst to be 99% so that you aren't eliminated from the competition. 0.99=1-(1-x)^3 0=0.01-(1-x)^3 and by factoring the difference of cubes we get 0=[100^(-1/3)-1+x]*[100^(-2/3)+(1-x)*100^(-1/3)+(1-x)^2] so 0=[100^(-1/3)-1+x] or 0=[100^(-2/3)+(1-x)*100^(-1/3)+(1-x)^2] the longer equation gives non-real roots so all we have to look at is 0=[100^(-1/3)-1+x] x=1-100^(-1/3) which is approximately 78.46% Similar to the 90% answer for a 2 solve round, 78.46% is the "break neck" speed. This is the fastest you can solve and still be almost certain that you won't be eliminated. -------------------------------- If anyone is interested in looking at this further I suggest graphing the two functions f(x)=1-(1-x)^2 and g(x)=1-(1-x)^3 and looking at where your typical success rate falls. In practice I tend to shoot for a 2/3 success rate, which is ok for a 3 solve competition (though I have a 1/20 chance of getting eliminated) but horrible for a 2 solve round (1/9 chance of getting eliminated). Anyway I was surprised by these results and wanted to post here in case anyone was interested. I think I'll start solving a little slower and more deliberately now :-) Chris
49. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Some math about competitive BLD solving

Hi Chris, Interesting post. Do you find that nerves play a role in competition blindfold solves which might reduce your success percentage - especially at breakneck speed?? If so, what about this for food for thought. Use the first solve to be more methodical. Don't go top speed but about 75%. Hopefully this will get you over your nerves and into the flow of blindsolving. Assuming a successful solve at 3/4 speed you already have your successful solve in the bank and can freely go at full speed for the other (two) solve(s) to try to get the fastest possible time. 50. Re: Some math about competitive BLD solving
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 04 Apr 2005 19:23:10 -0000

I didn't mention this in my first post, but I do something similar. The break neck speeds I list for each method are the speeds you should go until you get a successful solve. For a round with 2 solves you should solve with roughly 90% or better accuracy, so in words you are solving fairly slowly and carefully, but still taking some risks. This also, however, means you are going faster than perfect 100% accuracy where you take a long time. If you succeed on that solve, then for the final solve you should most definitely go whatever your most comfortable full speed is. Mine is roughly 66% accuracy which gives great times 2/3 the time, but obviously 1/3 aren't succesful. However, if you miss the first solve, you shouldn't freak out and slow down to solve with 100% accuracy on your second solve. By these odds you are already greater than 99% certain that you won't be eliminated if you solve the 2nd cube ALSO with 90% accuracy instead of getting nervous at having missed the 1st cube. ---- The same is true for 3 cubes, solve at 78-80% accuracy until you get a successful solve. If you miss the first 2 cubes, don't freak out and slow down to 100% accuracy. You can continue solving at 78-80% accuracy and you are still almost certain you won't be eliminated. Of course it is still possible that you will be eliminated, but that is the trade off for attempting to go faster than the slow and careful 100% accuracy type solving. I didn't mention that, but that was actually my intended observation with that math. Obviously if you get a good time on the first solve or within the first two for a 3 cube round, and you can happily accept that time as your fastest, then going full speed on the last few solves doesn't hurt at all. Chris --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, Rob Butler <futuese@y...> wrote: > > Hi Chris, > > Interesting post. Do you find that nerves play a role > in competition blindfold solves which might reduce > your success percentage - especially at breakneck > speed?? > > If so, what about this for food for thought. Use the > first solve to be more methodical. Don't go top speed > but about 75%. Hopefully this will get you over your > nerves and into the flow of blindsolving. Assuming a > successful solve at 3/4 speed you already have your > successful solve in the bank and can freely go at full > speed for the other (two) solve(s) to try to get the > fastest possible time. > > Just an idea, > > Rob

52. Re: Some math about competitive BLD solving

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > However, if you miss the first solve, you shouldn't freak out and > slow down to solve with 100% accuracy on your second solve. By > these odds you are already greater than 99% certain that you won't > be eliminated if you solve the 2nd cube ALSO with 90% accuracy > instead of getting nervous at having missed the 1st cube. > > ---- > > The same is true for 3 cubes, solve at 78-80% accuracy until you get > a successful solve. I don't think so. If you have just one solve left and have 79% accuracy then you'll have 79% chance to not be eliminated. Previous results don't matter and expectations you had before previous results don't matter either. Notice there's no Monty and more importantly there's no guaranteed car behind any door. Cheers! Stefan

54. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Some math about competitive BLD solving
From: Michael Zener Riggs Gottlieb <mzrg@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 04 Apr 2005 17:28:15 -0400

I don't think so. If you have just one solve left and have 79% accuracy then you'll have 79% chance to not be eliminated. Previous results don't matter and expectations you had before previous results don't matter either. Notice there's no Monty and more importantly there's no guaranteed car behind any door. If your entire tournament rests on one solve, and failing it would get you eliminated, you have two choices: go at a speed that makes you almost certain to do it correctly, or go as quickly as possible and hope everything goes nicely. You're not guaranteed to win with the second choice, but it depends if you want to be certain to get a mediocre solve or lucky to get a good one. What would *you* choose? --MZRG
55. Re: Some math about competitive BLD solving
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 04 Apr 2005 21:38:15 -0000

I agree, if you miss the first two solves and still solve at a faster rate (78-80% accuracy only) then for that last cube you only have a 78-80% chance of solving it. The reason I did this was to think of a strategy that allowed me to go as fast as possible (which is the same as the smallest accuracy possible) yet still not go too fast and get eliminated. My idea was to solve always with the same accuracy (roughly of course since that isn't possible exactly when doing a real group of BLD solves) and set that accuracy such that I have a very, very low chance of being eliminated. By your example I'm in a 3 solve round and have failed the first two solves. I agree that situation is possible, but there is a 24/25=96% chance that I would get at least one of the first two solves given an 80% solving accuracy. I also agree that having failed the first two solves, the chance of getting that last solve is still 80% and not 99%. I only mean that viewing all three solves as a group, and solving at 80%, the chance of missing all three is 0.8% I didn't say my strategy was perfect and that you would never be eliminated, I only meant it as a good goal to go for speed solving, yet not so fast that you are very likely to be eliminated. I meant my post as a compromise between speed, and being eliminted as infrequently as possible. 67. Re: GRRR!!
From: "Leyan Lo" <leyanlo@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 13 Apr 2005 08:18:15 -0000

What has helped me a lot, and what may help you, is to practice solving it "blindfolded" while looking at it. That is, memorize the cube, and then solve it using your blindfold method with your eyes open. This could help you realize silly errors that you are making in your blindfold method. After doing this for a while, I drastically improved my accuracy, and the solving time (after memorization) has shortened a bit. It's really cool when you can solve it blindfolded faster than you can solve it "blindfolded". Leyan --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "Bob Burton" <rubikscubewhiz@y...> wrote: > > Blindfold 3x3 Attempt #5 > > Memorization: 12 minutes > Execution: 8 minutes > > End result: two flipped edges, four twisted corners. All pieces > were permuted correctly. Had to fix the parity, so I know I did > that correctly. Getting so close that it's really really > frustrating me! The memorization is not a problem anymore...my > mistake is either: > > 1) memorizing the wrong orientation of some pieces > 2) doing the wrong setup alg for a piece i need to place (causing a > chain reaction and resulting in more flipped/twisted pieces) > > At least I've cut down on my memorization time (first attempt was 80 > minutes!) :-) At this rate, by the time I'm actually successful, it > will only take me a few seconds to memorize! :-[ > > Tomorrow is another day... > > ~ Bob

73. 2x2 cube algs
From: Andreas Renberg <rubikssites@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 18 Apr 2005 14:19:24 -0700 (PDT)

Does anyone know any 2x2 algs that swap 2 corners, 1 from the U layer, and 1 from D layer without orienting the corner pices incorrectly? --------------------------------- Do you Yahoo!? Yahoo! Mail - Find what you need with new enhanced search. Learn more.
74. Re: 2x2 cube algs
From: "Bob Burton" <rubikscubewhiz@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 18 Apr 2005 21:38:08 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, Andreas Renberg <rubikssites@y...> wrote: > Does anyone know any 2x2 algs that swap 2 corners, 1 from the U layer, and 1 from D layer without orienting the corner pices incorrectly? I'm sure there's something better than this, but this is what I use: (Certainly depending on which positions you would like to swap) This swaps ULB and DLF: F' (R U') (R' U' R U) (R' F') (R U R' U') (R' F R) F Notice F' is just a set-up move. I like this alg because it is the easy Y-permutation (without the first F move and last F') and it can be done very quickly. If you want to swap a different corner from the D-layer, use a different set-up move that doesn't move the ULB corner. Be sure to put the D-face of the cubie you are swapping in the RDF position. I hope this is clear? ~ Bob
75. Re: 2x2 cube algs
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 18 Apr 2005 22:55:29 -0000

How about: F (R U' R) D2 (R' U R) D2 R2 Shorter and also a good PLL alg ;-) Cheers! My favorite 2-by algorithm is (RUF)*5, which has the effect of removing the block of URF and DRF, turning it 180 degrees, and putting it back in. Try it first, though... --qqwref

Leyan
80. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] timer program
From: Michael Curtis <mick_curtis@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 22 Apr 2005 12:21:18 +0100 (BST)

In Excel, if you know how to do macros then do this: Sub time_and_down() Dim strV As String Selection.Value = VBA.Time strV = Right(ActiveCell.Address, 2) If Left(strV, 1) = "$" Then strV = Right(strV, 1) End If Cells(Val(strV) + 1, 1).Select End Sub You can set its options to have a short cut key where you press Ctrl and d. In that way, start at cell A1; each time you do Ctr + d, the current time will be displayed and the next cell down will be selected. It's a start. Mike --- Leyan Lo <leyanlo@...> wrote: > > > So I want to time each component of my blindfold > solve. Does anyone > know of a program that is able to mark off times > while keeping the > timer running? Like I want to be able to start the > timer, hit a > button when I'm done memorizing edges, hit a button > again when i'm > done memorizing corners, etc, until I complete the > solve, and then be > able to see all the times when I hit the button. > > Leyan > > > > > > > Yahoo! 88. Re: BLD 2-generator (on the 3x3x3)
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 01 May 2005 19:49:54 -0000

You use a normal 3x3x3 cube, but you are only allowed to turn two adjacent faces (usually U and R). So you would scramble with U and R, and also solve just by turning U and R. It makes for an interesting puzzle when doing it blindfolded. Chris P.S. 94. Re: Memorization
From: "Leyan Lo" <leyanlo@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 04 Jun 2005 21:10:50 -0000

Memorization order: EP CP CO EO Solve order: EO CO CP EP I memorize the edge and corner permutation with a combination of visual and muscle memory. I picture the cycles like Chun Hei says, by "drawing imaginary lines for edges and corners" and trace these cycles out with my hands to enforce my memory. There are tricks to this, like associating certain patterns with something else: fish shaped cycles, triangle patterns, cycles that jump all the way around, symmetrical cycles, cycles that bounce diagonally across the whole cube, or just across one face, etc. For corner orienation, I use numbers or words. I actually repeat what I memorized out loud, and keep on repeating them while looking at the edge orientation. That way, I can better remember what I memorized by remembering what I was hearing myself say. I don't really memorize edge orientation, as that is the first step I solve after I put on the blindfold. I just memorize what setup moves I need to do and what algorithms I'm going to use. Leyan --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "yhgrps_m" <moxyd@f...> wrote: > Hi, > > After a few solves, I found it was much less painful for me to > memorize the initial state using only my "visual memory". > Do you always use numbers for memorization? I prefer drawing the > cycles in my head, even if it is a little longer. > > I once tried to remember corner permutation by singing different > pitches, it could work, but i need some training :-) > > Have you tried any different techniques ? > > > Marc
95. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Memorization
From: Kåre Krig <karkr936@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 05 Jun 2005 12:51:16 +0200

I memorize permutations (cycles) by visualisation. I have improved my memorization twice since the first approach. I guess most people figure out the same tricks but here it goes EP = (13479a2b56)(8)(c), just remove 1-cycles 1. Pair up the pieces. (13 47 9a 2b 56) Pairs are almost as easy to remember as a single piece and just half as many. 2. When I permute using 3-cycles my I cycle the first three pieces, solving the second and third. Having to break up pairs all the time is just stupid and time consuming so have an odd number of pieces in the first group. (134 79 a2 b5 6) For CO I memorize the pairs (or tripplets) to be solved with one alg. For EO I remember setup moves and the algs to use. Visual memorization works, and is ok for the 3x3x3. But if you want to try bigger cubes you should try other ways. Pochmann has a nice way that I'm trying out for bigger cubes http://www.stefan-pochmann.de/spocc/blindsolving/3x3/ Pochmann also solve the 3x3 one piece at a time (permutation and orientation simultaneously). Anyone tried to solve two pieces at a time like this? This should save lots of turns, but mabey the setup moves make it to hard/slow? /Kåre ----- Original Message ----- From: yhgrps_m <moxyd@...> Date: Saturday, June 4, 2005 10:36 pm Subject: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Memorization > Hi, > > After a few solves, I found it was much less painful for me to > memorize the initial state using only my "visual memory". > Do you always use numbers for memorization? I prefer drawing the > cycles in my head, even if it is a little longer. > > I once tried to remember corner permutation by singing different > pitches, it could work, but i need some training :-) > > Have you tried any different techniques ? > > > Marc >
96. Re: Memorization
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 05 Jun 2005 11:28:51 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, Kåre Krig <karkr936@s...> wrote: > > Pochmann also solve the 3x3 one piece at a time (permutation and > orientation simultaneously). Anyone tried to solve two pieces at a time > like this? This should save lots of turns, but mabey the setup moves > make it to hard/slow? I thought about solving two edges at a time. My buffer edge (from where I solve) would be DF. Then I'd bring the other pieces to UF and UB (or maybe UL or UR). Depending on the orientations of these two pieces, I'd choose one out of four algs. If you learn 8 algs then you can also choose which one goes to UF and which to UB. All this should lead to quite short and easy setup moves (2+2 moves is a trivial upper bound but I think 3 might actually be the worst case). The easiest case/alg of course would be [U2, M'] but the others aren't that bad, either. Cheers! Stefan

104. Re: what the hell?
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 13 Jun 2005 13:07:13 -0000

I really have been trying to think how that's possible. There are only two ways I can think to do this. 1) Memorize cycles, but also simultaneously which pieces are flipped. So when you come to a flipped piece in the cycle you just memorize that it is flipped. Giving thought to 20 pieces would mean doing 2.5 pieces per second. Seems possible with lots of practice. 2) The other way, and I think this is less likely to be the way he did it, is to memorize the pieces at DBL, DL, DB, BL and also their flip. Then you can just visually scan the UFR faces for their colors long enough to get an idea where the pieces are and their flips. After you put on the blindfold you would first spend lots of time decoding the UFR faces, then you could solve by cycles. I mean I guess it's possible but it sees almost unreasonable. Chris --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "Bob Burton" <rubikscubewhiz@y...> wrote: > 8 seconds to memorize?!?!?

106. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: what the hell?
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 13 Jun 2005 08:53:35 -0600

I think he still uses Stefan's method. I'm wondering how much time he spends after memorization decoding the information before he solves because his solve times are pretty slow. Memorization is a tool; it is not a gift. It becomes strengthened with practice. The key thing to remember is the he focuses on memory, whereas we're cubers. I'm pretty sure we'll always hold the advantage as long as we can regularly solve the cube in about 90 seconds. Even if our memorization takes one minute, 2:30 is still a ridiculously good time. As cubers, we never train our memories, because it hasn't gotten there yet. Currently, people are still working on solving faster, and retaining the information they memorized. I don't know if any of us can memorize the cube in 8 seconds, but memorizing the cube in 8 seconds is absolutely useless if it takes you 30 seconds before you start the solve. Furthermore, our fathers are not John Louis. I'm pretty sure that we can train ourselves to memorize the cube in about 30 seconds, reasonably. I mean, I've been blindfold cubing for about 2 weeks, and orientation only takes maybe 10 seconds to memorize right? And, with some practice, I know I could remember 20 numbers at one number per second... I was trying this before with 10 numbers on the computer on the memory olympiad page. The key is to build confidence in your memory, that you remember the pieces in each cycle, maybe say them once in your head, and then never go back, and you start, and you're confident that you have it right. After these memory accomplishments, blindfold cubers will start to focus on memory now, because we know it's possible. I would just practice some speed memory exercises. Other things I would do, and I do this myself, is delete all phone numbers in your stupid cell phone, and memorize them instead. If you're stranded somewhere, and you don't have your cell phone, it'll save your butt. 109. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: what the hell?
From: Kåre Krig <karkr936@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 14 Jun 2005 11:33:04 +0200

The downside with Pochmanns method is that it's not verry good for solving orientation and permutation separately like most of us do now. But if you learn a bunch of algs to solve two pieces (orient + permute) at a time it should be really fast. My experiments with images and routes shows that its the way to go for bigger cubes (mabye all bigger than 2x2) because time consumption scales almost linear to the amount of data to memorize. I think the top memorizers do a deck of cards in 20-25s. That is about the same amount of data as a 4x4x4. So 8s for a 3x3x3 is really fast but I still belive it can be done. /Kåre ----- Original Message ----- From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...> Date: Monday, June 13, 2005 9:11 pm Subject: Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: what the hell? > In that case, we should expect blindfold records to reach under 1 > minute at some point! > > Tyson Mao > MSC #631 > California Institute of Technology > > On Jun 13, 2005, at 9:14 AM, Stefan Pochmann wrote: > > > Yes, Bernett still uses my method. 110. Re: what the hell?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 14 Jun 2005 11:03:09 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, Kåre Krig <karkr936@s...> wrote: > > The downside with Pochmanns method is that it's not verry good for > solving orientation and permutation separately That's not a downside, that's an intention. I wouldn't even want to do them separately. > I think the top memorizers do a deck of cards in 20-25s. As far as I know the official *record* is still 32.9 seconds: http://www.recordholders.org/en/list/memory.html#cards-speed > That is about the same amount of data as a 4x4x4. It feels a bit strange to compare these. For the cubes we usually analyze/memorize the structure of the scramble, i.e. the cycles. Andy memorizes a deck of cards by always just taking the next three cards and memorizing them as one mental image (with subject, action and object). There's no analysis whatsoever, it's pure raw memorization (after translating the cards to subject/action/object but that's automatic with lots of practice). So he memorizes about 17 images in 30+ seconds, whereas as far as I know Bernett memorized around 20 in 8 seconds, though his are simpler (only one item at each place, not three). For the 4x4 compared to the 3x3 we also have another difficulty, namely pieces that look the same (the centers) and we have to decide where each should go. That's actually my biggest trouble solving the larger cubes. A better comparison might be between 3x3 and the record for memorizing digits in 4 seconds, where the record is 22. Gert certainly translates at least 2 digits as one image and probably stores the last few images in short term memory rather than connecting them with a mental route. So that's 11 minus a few images in four seconds. Oh, and wherever I said "image", that's not necessarily only visual. It can be more like a scene with smells, movement, sounds, feeling, tastes etc. Anything can help make the memory stronger, though it might take more time. Cheers! Stefan
111. New guy in town
From: "reusto" <aruestow@...>

116. Any Interest in Other Forms of Blindfold Cubing? How Many Forms are There?
From: "blindfoldcubist" <jmittan@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 21 Jun 2005 04:01:22 -0000

If you are a member of this group, you are certainly familiar with the competition form of blindfold cubing (solver examines the cube, puts on a blindfold then solves the cube). Every so often one hears of other forms of blindfold cubing. The other forms require the assistance at least one other person. In one form, the sighted person tells the blindfolded person which moves to make, one move at a time. Some have enhanced this variation to allow the sighted person to tell the blindfolded person which algorithm to perform. And in at least one case, a cube has been solved over the phone. The person making the moves described the cube to the other person, who indicated which moves to make. At WC2003, I demonstrated a version in which I was blindfolded, and was assisted by a helper. After the cube was scrambled, the helper picked a color. I pointed at squares, and was told "yes" or "no" depending on whether or not I was pointing at the selected color. This information was used to place all squares of the selected on one side. Next, the helper picked a second color, and "yes" and "no" questions were again used to assemble the second side without destroying the first side. Then a third, fourth and fifth color were chosen, and in each case, a new side was completed without destroying the previously completed sides. (There is never a need to pick the sixth side, because completion of the fifth side forces the sixth side to be solved as well. The squares of the sixth color have no other place to be other than the one remaining side.) Over the course of many years I have developed many variations of the method that I demonstrated at WC2003. These are finally described in a Word document that I am placing in the files section. You will also find an Excel spreadsheet that contrasts the various methods. So please take a look, and let me know your questions and comments. If there is enough interest, I am willing to describe how some of these variations are accomplished, starting with "basic seeing eye blindfold" cubing. Above all, if you know of still other variations, by all means let me know. Thanks. Jim Mittan

They've got some pretty cool names for their algorithms too. 118. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Parity Errors
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 20 Jun 2005 22:43:35 -0600

Hey Adam, Let's say you have a parity with corners and edges. In other words, you want to switch two corners and two edges. If the two corners aren't diagonally in the same layer, I first do a T-permutation to fix those two corners. If one corner is in the top layer and the other corner is in the bottom layer, in the process of putting the corner from the bottom to the top, you need to be careful not to touch the edges that will be switched in the T-permutation. So then, I put the cube back, and I remember which two corners are switched. I then just place to the two edges in the opposite slots, and do an H-permutation (four-edge swap across) and that solves it. There are other things you can do but the T-permutation + H-permutation is really fast for setting up and very fast for executing. Tyson Mao MSC #631 California Institute of Technology On Jun 20, 2005, at 7:26 PM, adamb0mb024 wrote: > I just started learning blinfold solving and I'm trying to get the > method down, but I keep running inot problems with parity errors at > the very end.  Can someone give me advice on how to work them out? > -AK > > > > Yahoo! 121. Re: wow....
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 21 Jun 2005 09:41:51 -0000

He's only doing 2-cycles. I've always known it's fast but nobody believed it ;-) Besides being easy, it's also fast because the algorithms are very fast and the setup moves are short (around 1-2 moves) and there are so few of them that you can *know* them all, there's *no thinking* required once you practiced enough and you can do it subconsciously. And Bernett does use finger tricks, sure. Shortly after he recently lubed his cube for the first time (and watched a T-perm video with finger tricks), he did the T-perm four times in 12-16 seconds and we all know that's slow, so he might be faster now that he has practiced with a lubed cube (haven't asked him since). The other three algorithms are very fast, too. Cheers! 129. Orienting and permuting at the same time
From: "yhgrps_m" <moxyd@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 24 Jun 2005 11:48:03 -0000

Instead of orienting, and then permuting pieces using setup moves within a group which preserves orientation, has anyone tried to use setup moves to orient pieces? It seems easy for edges, a little more complicated for corners, and of course, you can't always orient all the pieces you are permuting. Marc
130. Re: Orienting and permuting at the same time
From: "yhgrps_m" <moxyd@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 25 Jun 2005 15:30:47 -0000

Here is a way to solve edges for Macky's example solve. (http://cubefreak.hp.infoseek.co.jp/blindfoldcubing.html#ex333) I use a different definition of a "correct" orientation: my "edge group" is <R,L,U,D,F2,B2>. Scramble: D' B' F R' F2 U F L2 D2 B' U2 R2 D' L2 F R' D' F U L' F U' R B2 U' EP: (1 7 9 12 11 4 5 8)(2 6) EO: 1 2 3 4 5 6 7 10 11 12 (1 7 9), flip 1 7 D2BR - UFB' R2 F'BU - R'B'D2 (1 12 11), flip 1 12 R'BU'BU - R2 UFB' R2 F'BU R2 - U'B'UB'R (1 4 5), flip 1 4 RBL - U'F'B L2 FBU' - L'B'R' (1 8) (2 6), flip 1 8 2 6 U'B'U'F' - M2UM2U2M2UM2 - FUBU flip 3 10 DF2 - M'UM'UM'U2MUMUMU2 - F2D' This is just an example. Given the number of edges to flip, I would do it differently. Marc
131. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Orienting and permuting at the same time
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sun, 26 Jun 2005 01:03:33 -0600

I kind of feel that the setup moves, and keeping track of what edges need to be flipped would slow you down. I have no idea, of course, whether or not you would make up for it by not doing the edge orientation, but in my experience, edge orientation takes 5 seconds to memorize (if you're human) and maybe 10 seconds to execute... and once those are all oriented "correctly" by whatever group you want, well, the setup moves for permutation are all so easy that pretty much there's no thinking involved. I guess the only way to see how it works is to get a bunch of people trying it as well. 137. Re: Orienting and permuting at the same time
From: "mackymakisumi" <mackymakisumi@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 27 Jun 2005 00:36:18 -0000

Maybe something like this: 000 000 0 001 000 1 010 001 1 011 001 0 100 100 1 101 100 0 110 010 0 111 010 1 The first group of three letters are the orientation of the edges you are cycling, in that order, where 0 means correct and 1 means incorrect. The second group indicate whether the corresponding edge in the first group should be untouched (0) or flipped (1) before permuting. Notice that it is always possible to incorrect insert just one, which can be achieved by a set-up move with an odd number of R/L set-up moves. The last number is the orientation you are left with at the end for the first edge. The second and the third will be solved. Again, that's a quick idea, but it's pretty simple which edge must be flipped in each case. Macky --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "mackymakisumi" <mackymakisumi@y...> wrote: > 3-edge cycle PLL's are so easy that it almost seems like a waste to add > slower algorithms. I'd rather experiment with set-up moves to control > orientation. > > Anyway, I also think orientation & permutation at the same time using 3- > cycles is definitely worth a try, at least for the edges. > > Macky >

141. Stefan system, sample solve
From: smoothcuber <no_reply@yahoogroups.com>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 29 Jun 2005 01:00:18 -0000

Hey, I've just started learning Stefan's system and would like to know if I'm understanding it correctly. So here's a sample solve; please let me know if it's all wrong, and go easy. Scramble: F'DR L2FU' LB'U2 BD'F' R'B2D2 L2F'R UL2D' FRFU' The four moves used: Move A: L'U RU' LU2 R'UR U2R'- (swaps UFR-UBR, UR-UF) Move B: LU' R'UL' U2 RU'R' U2R -(swaps UFR-UBR, UR-UB) Move C: FRU' R'UR UR2 F'R UR U'R' - T perm Move E: RU'R' U' RUR' F'R UR'U' R'FR - (swaps UBL-DFR, UB-UL) Edges: Target slot UR - (setup moves in parentheses) Move 1: (M)move B(M')- 13 turns Move 2: (M')move B(M)- 13 turns Move 3: (L)move C(L')- 16 turns Move 4: (E2L)move C(L'E2)- 18 turns Move 5: move C - 14 turns Move 6: (EL)move C(L'E') - 18 turns Move 7: (L2)move C(L2) - 16 turns Move 8: (D'M')move A(MD) - 15 turns Move 9: move B - 11 turns Move 10: (M')move A(M) - 13 turns Move 11: (M)move A(M') - 13 turns Total turns for edges: 160 Corners: Target slot DFR, from UBL Move 1: Parity fix, PLL move: y U'R2 B'R'U' R'URBR' U2R U2R' y' - 14 turns Move 2: (D)move E(D') - 17 turns Move 3: (F2)move E(F2) - 17 turns Move 4: (RD')move E(DR') - 19 turns Move 5: (R')move E(R) - 17 moves Move 6: (DF')move E(FD') - 19 turns Move 7: (R)move E(R') - 17 turns Move 8: (RF)move E(F'R') - 17 turns Total turns for corners: 139 Total turns for entire solve: 299 I hope no typos, -Dave
142. Re: Stefan system, sample solve
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 29 Jun 2005 11:19:52 -0000

Hi Dave, isn't your success (I solved the cube using your instructions) proof enough that you did it right? ;-) There are some improvements though that I can suggest: - Do your T-perm backwards, I think that's much faster. - Do the MoveA-PLL backwards, I think that's faster, too. - Use double-layer instead of slice moves for setup. - Instead of edges move11 and corners move1 you could do: y (r U R' U' M U R U' R') y' That's just 10 instead of 27 moves. But I only recently thought about shortcutting the parity. - For corners try other PLLs than MoveE, e.g in move4 I'd now do U+MoveB+U'. 145. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Corner's Algorithm
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 30 Jun 2005 09:57:52 -0600

I probably would have done it using T-permutations. Okay, so first switch UFL and DFB: U2 F2 (T-permutation) F2 U2. We have to keep in mind that UL and UR corners are switched. Now, switch UBL and DBR: U2 D B2 (T-permutation) B2 D' U2 and that should do it. Sometimes, if you do a U2 or no setup move and then a U' or U setup move in the other one, you just have to do a four edge opposite swap. These algorithms, are fortunately, all very very fast. What do you guys think? 146. Re: Corner's Algorithm
From: "yhgrps_m" <moxyd@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 30 Jun 2005 17:17:57 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "phatydee555" <dbergman@i...> wrote: > DBR. So, i was left at the end of my corner permutation with UFL - > DFB and UBL with DBR and for some reason can't figure out a set up > move for this. Do you really mean DFB? With cases like this, I often use two 3-cycles. The first one puts a piece in its correct place, the second one solves the three others. I requires not much thinking, which is always a good thing for me, because I tend to forget what I memorized when I spend too much time finding a difficult setup move. To solve (UFL DFL)(UBL DBR): (UFL UBL DBR) then (DFL UFL UBL) Marc

162. New Cube Demo With Input Method Change + Help Screen
From: "pyjamamitten" <jpittman@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 25 Aug 2005 15:08:47 -0000

Greetings, We want to thank everyone from the online cubing community who has been kind enough to send us their comments, ideas, and suggestions. Over the past week, we have accumulated some great ideas from you folks as to how we can make our product better! Please keep sending us feedback and continue to try out the new versions as we post them. The biggest change in the new version is related to the comments we've received about wanting an alternate method of orienting your view of the cube. So, without losing the existing freedom of rotation, we have added a secondary way to change your view of the cube. Holding down the left shift key while rotating a slice of the cube will turn the *entire* cube 90 degrees (or 180 if you also have the left CTRL key down) in the same axis/direction the single slice would have moved. This eases the ability to 'flip' the cube around and see the backside without losing your current perspective on the cube. Thanks to Joël van Noort for this suggestion! An in-game help screen has been added that details all the available controls as well. The change log for the demo project is now posted on the downloads page of our website (http://www.gabbasoft.com) if anyone is interested in seeing everything that has been modified to date. Again, thanks so much for all of your input and we hope you will continue to send us your thoughts and ideas for making our product better. Regards, Jamey Pittman President GabbaSoft, Inc. jpittman@... http://www.gabbasoft.com
163. Cube Demo Updated For 20x20x20 Cube Support
From: "pyjamamitten" <jpittman@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 27 Aug 2005 03:36:45 -0000

Greetings, I can't believe how many requests we've received about the desire for larger cubes! Of course, I should have known better after I saw that someone in Japan had solved the 9x9x9 in a little over 2 hours and posted the screenshots on his site. So, as of this release, we now support cube sizes up to 20x20x20. I would certainly expect that performance will noticably degrade on slower/older systems when you get up to 15x15x15 and above but you are free to give `em a try. Ton from Speedcubing.com made the request for changing the timer behavior to wait until the first move is made after a scramble to start  seemed to be a good idea to me so that has been added as well. We've also received enough comments about the moving background making people dizzy to change it and make it static  we hope you like it better! You can get the latest version from our downloads page: http://www.gabbasoft.com/download.htm As always, thanks so much for all of your input and we hope you will continue to send us your thoughts and ideas for making our product better. Regards, Jamey Pittman President GabbaSoft, Inc. jpittman@... http://www.gabbasoft.com

166. Edge permutation - problems on memorising
From: "pedrosino1" <pedrosino1@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 13 Sep 2005 21:13:26 -0000

Hey, people, I'm relatively new to BLD cubing, I think 2 weeks of practicing, and I'm having some troubles with memorisation of edges permutation. Sometimes, I do CO, EO, CP and when I get to EP, I think: Well, what was the cycle? And sometimes I can't remember, and when I think I'm finished, the cube is not solved... There's a good way to memorise EP? I usually do like this: for example, a cycle (1 4 7 6 2 9 11 10 8 12 3) will be broken into (1 4 7, 6 2 9, 11 10 8, 12 3) but sometimes I forgot the numbers... do you think it's a good idea to broke into something like this: (1 4 7, 6 2, 9 11, 10 8, 12 3)??? that way, I cut 4 and 7, do 1 6 2, cut 6 and 2, do 1 9 11, cut 9 and 11... Pedro
167. Re: Edge permutation - problems on memorising
From: "mgwallisa" <mattwallisa@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 13 Sep 2005 23:38:56 -0000

OK, I've been doing this for a couple weeks now, too. I started learning it with three cycles, but kept messing up somewhere in the setup moves, so I've been keeping it all just two cycles with the T permutation, thanks to Bill McGaugh. It seems to also be easier for me in memorization because I just see the numbers as an individual pair. I do the same T permutation for the corners, but keep the numbers straight by remembering them as a whole number, so 1 3 on the edges I would think as "one, three", but on the corners as "thirteen". Also, I memorize the edge permutation first because it's the longest string and I can make sure I have it down before I start to solve. I memorize the corner permutation last because it's just a few numbers that I'll get too real soon. The orientations I do in between. I'll start again with a faster solving solution in a few weeks, once memorizing isn't what slows me down most. Right now I'm keeping it real simple. I don't know if any of that would help, I guess memorizing is just something that gets easier with practice. It was taking me about nine minutes at first to memorize a couple weeks ago, now it's about four minutes. Good luck, Matt --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "pedrosino1" <pedrosino1@y...> wrote: > Hey, people, > I'm relatively new to BLD cubing, I think 2 weeks of practicing, and > I'm having some troubles with memorisation of edges permutation. > Sometimes, I do CO, EO, CP and when I get to EP, I think: Well, what > was the cycle? And sometimes I can't remember, and when I think I'm > finished, the cube is not solved... > > There's a good way to memorise EP? I usually do like this: > for example, a cycle (1 4 7 6 2 9 11 10 8 12 3) will be broken into > (1 4 7, 6 2 9, 11 10 8, 12 3) > but sometimes I forgot the numbers... > do you think it's a good idea to broke into something like this: > (1 4 7, 6 2, 9 11, 10 8, 12 3)??? > that way, I cut 4 and 7, do 1 6 2, cut 6 and 2, do 1 9 11, cut 9 and > 11... > > Pedro
168. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Edge permutation - problems on memorising
From: Sachin Shirwalkar <sachin_civilian@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 14 Sep 2005 08:02:01 +0100 (BST)

Im just 2-3 weeks into BLD now, but a trick i use has made my memorisation relatively solid for me. What i do isthat along with numbers i try to remember the perms visually. Like some diagonal corners or opposite edges something like that. And i make sure i know the first 2-3 numbers perfectly. becoz when i recollect the first sequence, the whole cycle comes to me immediately. Hope this helps. 169. Re: Edge permutation - problems on memorising
From: "yhgrps_m" <moxyd@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 14 Sep 2005 12:18:57 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "pedrosino1" <pedrosino1@y...> wrote: > Hey, people, > I'm relatively new to BLD cubing, I think 2 weeks of practicing, and > I'm having some troubles with memorisation of edges permutation. > Sometimes, I do CO, EO, CP and when I get to EP, I think: Well, what > was the cycle? And sometimes I can't remember, and when I think I'm > finished, the cube is not solved... Have you tried to try to check that you remember EP after each step when you are solving? It may help, especially if you're not very fast during the solve. > There's a good way to memorise EP? I usually do like this: > for example, a cycle (1 4 7 6 2 9 11 10 8 12 3) will be broken into > (1 4 7, 6 2 9, 11 10 8, 12 3) > but sometimes I forgot the numbers... > do you think it's a good idea to broke into something like this: > (1 4 7, 6 2, 9 11, 10 8, 12 3)??? > that way, I cut 4 and 7, do 1 6 2, cut 6 and 2, do 1 9 11, cut 9 and > 11... I agree with Sachin Shirwalkar, you should try to memorize EP visually, noticing patterns in the cycles. This has been discussed in this thread: http://games.groups.yahoo.com/group/blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube/message/301 I sometimes imagine that I am on a roller coaster going from cubie to cubie :) Anyway, I think you should not memorize numbers as if they had no "structure", like in a telephone number, but always see their position on the cube.

177. Re: w00w00
From: "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 10 Oct 2005 20:58:30 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, mike_go_uk <no_reply@y...> wrote: > > --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "thomkirjava" > <snkenjoi@g...> wrote: > > thinking of a possable way to > > tackle the square-1. (It'd be so simple if it was always a cube) > > Yes... "easy" once it's in the cube shape, but following every piece > through the shape transformations to cube seems painful. Or do you > have something else in mind? > > Mike > I have had different theories for a while. I'm constantly trying to develop ideas for the puzzles on my bld wishlist. basically, My first idea was find an alg that swap two pieces whatever they are. I came up with this: -2,0/-1,-1/1,-2/-3,-3/-3,-4/3,0/-6,-5/3,-2/-1,0/-6,-4/5,-5 This will swap a corner and edge piece. If this worked all the time, you could easily solve the square-1 piece by piece using 2-cycles. Problem is, it won't work all the time. ie; if the squ1 is in a weird shape, the alg won't work. My first idea was to learn the algs for piece swapping for all shapes. After hasty reconsideration I decided that it'd be much easyer to find an alg that works on every shape somehow that moves just two or three pieces around. This cannot be done with standard notation. I'm thinking, 'move top layer two cuts(/pieces?) along, bottom one cut along and twist' Or find one alg you can use all the time, only in the middle, it changed depending on the shape of the top layer, then you'd only need to learn one alg and a few numbers to change depending on the shapes. Or course, there is surly a much easyer way. I can't be bothered arbitarily remembering where the pieces are every turn. Maybe Lars V. can help shed some light on this situation. ~Thom
178. Re: w00w00
From: mike_go_uk <no_reply@yahoogroups.com>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 11 Oct 2005 12:43:59 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@g...> wrote: > My first idea was to learn the algs for piece swapping for all shapes. ... > Or find one alg you can use all the time, Maybe there's a middle way, where you would convert your given shape to one of a small number standard shapes (whichever is closest), apply the alg to that standard shape, and then convert back again. But it doesn't seem very hopeful: after all, swapping an edge and a corner /changes/ the shape. The brute-force "method" of following the pieces through the shape transformations mightn't be too bad if one always used the same sequence to restore a given shape, and had a clear picture of that sequence in one's head... but unfortunately I don't. :( Mike

180. Re: w00w00
From: "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 11 Oct 2005 17:54:44 -0000

Ok, I'm starting to think this isn't such a bad idea. To swap two pieces in any shape; Apply moves to turn into cube. (Remember pieces you wanna swap are) Apply setup moves to put the pieces into places for the alg. Do Swappy-move. Apply inverse setup moves. Apply moves to change into original shape. Problem: because you've swapped the pieces, the inverse of the changing to cube shape will require different moves. If we can solve the above problem, the Squ1BLD looks in reach. Possable improvment: Find an alg to swap two pieces that only turns the top layer. Then you only have to turn one layer of the squ1 into a cube shape to execute. ~Thom --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@g...> wrote: > > > Maybe there's a middle way, where you would convert your given shape to > > one of a small number standard shapes (whichever is closest), apply the > > alg to that standard shape, and then convert back again. 182. Copying a Scrambled Cube Blindfolded
From: "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 22 Oct 2005 19:52:24 -0000

About 10 mins ago I accomplished this on the first try. Here's the text I sent to the speedcubing.com records (in case it doesn't get a new category made) "Name: Thom Barlow Email: snkenjoi@gmailNOSPAMcom Puzzle: Rubik's 3x3x3 Cube Type: Single Solve Category: Blindfold: Copying a Scrambled Cube Blindfolded. Result: 13:59.51 Quote: Rules: Get two cubes, scramble one of them, start timer. Look at scrambled cube for a while, memorise it. Put on blindfold. Do moves so the solved cube looks like the scrambled one. Take off blindfold. Scramble: R' B' R2 B2 R' U' D F2 U2 F2 L' R B2 F' U' F' D2 L' R2 U' B2 F2 D' B' F' - Well, this is a hell of a lot of fun. Slightly harder though. Here is a short description of what to do (in addition to the 3-cycles method); Do permutation then orientation, do orientation of corners as normal. For corner orientation, memorise it so that you don't do the move to fix the orientation, you do the move to get it to that orientation. Permutation: Memorise the cycles backwards, excluding the first number. Eg: 18572 becomes 12758. When fixing parity/cycling two set of corners, you can leave it alone. Eg: 34 is still 34. ~Kirjava" Explantion is up there. Try it, it's fun. ~Thom

This year, each competition (total 4 times in a year-this one is fourth competition) is going to have 10 puzzles and these puzzle competitions are open to all country. Competitions will be published in only Turkish, English and Japanese. You will have exactly 7 days to submit the answers of the puzzles via "Answer" form page, beginning from the puzzle's publishing date. You should read information about answer format in puzzle text carefully before answering! Each correct answer is 100 points and additionally there are two types of bonus points: One of the bonus points are given to the first 3 competitors who give the correct answer (30, 20 and 10 point) and the other bonus points are given depending on answering day, beginning from current puzzle's publishing date (30, 25, 20, 15, 10 and 5 point). Your score will be updated as soon as you post your answer. You can see whether your answer is correct or not on the "Score Analyze Table" instantly. Correct answers and solutions will be given when the next puzzle is published. 191. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] I have a lack of confidence :S
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 28 Oct 2005 14:12:01 -0700

I would break it up into two steps: First, do U2 F2. This puts the 5 corner next to the 1 corner on the right side. Do a T-permutation that switches those two corners. Then do F2 U2 to put it back. Then, bring the 10 edge up by doing D F2. Do the H-permutation which switches all four edges. You'll correct 3 and 10, and then fix 2 and 4 which were messed up in the T-permutation. I do almost all of my parity fixes with something like that. It's basically instant because you first think about corners, and then consider edges. 193. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] I have a lack of confidence :S
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 28 Oct 2005 18:42:19 -0700

That's true, but setting up PLLs like that only work for certain cases and you can do them if you "see" it immediately but if it takes you time to figure it out, you're better off going with a 2-step no-delay method. Tyson Mao MSC #631 California Institute of Technology On Oct 28, 2005, at 5:59 PM, Pedro wrote: > Well, I would do D2 R2 U2 and the R permutation. Then do U2 R2 D2 and > you're done... >   > Pedro > > Tyson Mao <tmao@its.caltech.edu> escreveu: >> I would break it up into two steps: >> >> First, do U2 F2.  This puts the 5 corner next to the 1 corner on the >> right side.  Do a T-permutation that switches those two corners.  Then >> do F2 U2 to put it back. >> >> Then, bring the 10 edge up by doing D F2.  Do the H-permutation which >> switches all four edges.  You'll correct 3 and 10, and then fix 2 and >> 4 >> which were messed up in the T-permutation. >> >> I do almost all of my parity fixes with something like that.  It's >> basically instant because you first think about corners, and then >> consider edges. >> >> Tyson Mao >> MSC #631 >> California Institute of Technology >> >> On Oct 28, 2005, at 2:02 PM, thomkirjava wrote: >> >> > Ok, I just landed a BLD solve at 14:xx.xx or soemthing... >> > >> >  I think I have a big problem executing my algs without seeing them >> >  executed. >> > >> >  I was scared that I'd mess up the '+' PLL to setup some edges for a >> >  prity fix, so I ended up doing three T-perms to fix it all :S >> > >> >  The edges were at 3 and 10, and the corners were at 1 and 5. 199. Interesting...
From: "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 09 Nov 2005 19:21:52 -0000

it seems pochmann's system does me well, well, to an extent. I'm still using cycles for the corners, but using his method for edges is nice. I don't like the memorisation technique, so I do it differently. I remember everything spatially, ie. no number/letters/colours/pictures. And I just memorise which stickers to put in the target position in order. This feels amazingly fast for me and I can remember it faster than a sequence of potentionally 12 numbers or colours. If edges are in the right place but flipped, I just remember to flip them with the buffer edge at the end, or with another flipped in place cubie. The other fun thing is the parity. If I can solve the corners fully, I know there is none, but if two corners will be left flipped, I know there will be parity, so I just flip the corners back and flip the two in the buffer position at the same time. Then i can ignore anything pertaining to parity really, I don't even have to count the length of the cycles of edges :) You lot probibly already know all this but I thought I'd share. I got excited after I began to be able to memorise edges faster than corners :P. Fastest I've solved now is 7:14 and I got a 5:38 DNF where two corners were twisted. Thanks, ~Thom

201. Can you exercise memory?
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 11 Nov 2005 07:01:59 -0000

Hey everyone, Lately I've been trying to work on using my memory in situtations where I usually wouldn't. For example I'm reading a book right now, and rather than use a bookmark I just remember the page number I am at when I stop. I've found that it takes less mental effort to remember the number and I can still recall it later when I come back, even if I don't get a chance to read for a few days. Also for a few months now I have been memorizing friends phone numbers rather than putting them into my phone (I saw that suggestion from Tyson a while back somewhere). Do little things like this improve memory/recall? Specifically do they help for BLD cubing? Or is it just wishful thinking on my part? Chris
202. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Can you exercise memory?
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 10 Nov 2005 23:15:10 -0800

Hi Chris, I've been working on "one-pass" memorization for memorizing the permutation. Right now I'm a bit slower at it, but I see the potential the memorize the entire permutation of the cube in 20 seconds or less. I believe that the average memorization time of the cube can be done in average of 30 seconds or less. The idea of one-pass memorization is that you only go through the cycles once and after that, you're done. I've been able to do it for maybe 60% of the cubes I try to solve if I go slowly. Of course, my times right now are a bit slower because I'm incorporating new things, but I think the memorization time of the permutations is starting to become equal... and I'm going through at a slow rate for the permutation memorization right now. Perhaps try breaking down the cube into smaller parts and seeing if you can remember those parts with less and less study time. Memorizing a page in a book is certainly different. You only need to hold the information of a cube for about 60 seconds... or until you solve that part of the cube. I think the memory olympiads have some java applets you can use to practice memorizing digits of numbers that flash by at a rate of 1 per second. I should give that another shot. Though, it does seem like I'm the only person left in the world who uses numbers. Though, I'm sure it can't hurt to remember the license plate number of that idiot in the green Honda SUV who cut you off on the highway... or the Pasadena police car who was parked in a handicapped spot. I think it was 1007069... but that was like, maybe half a year ago. 208. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Can you exercise memory?
From: JohnLouis Louis <pjlmem@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 11 Nov 2005 16:21:40 +0000 (GMT)

You have to see this way. For example, if you have to permute edges in the order, say , 851094, instead of remembering so many meaningless numbers, you can remember one meaningful word - feldspar- which will tell you the numbers in the same order. Numbers can not be visualized . So it is difficult to remember. But meaningful words can be remembered much better. Number the pieces. Check the order by which it has to be solved. To remember the order, convert them into meaningful words. Decode the words into numbers when you are actually solving the cube. John Louis mike_go_uk <no_reply@yahoogroups.com> wrote: --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, JohnLouis Louis <pjlmem@y...> wrote: > > Talc = 75 > gypsum = 6903 > feldspar = 851094 > topaz = 190 > corundum = 74213 > diamond = 1321. > using phonetic sound and value system, any number can be converted into any meaningful word or vice versa. Hi John, Unfortunately this is nowhere near fast enough for blindfold solving. If we want to memorize the cube in 40 seconds, we don't have time to transpose the group of numbers into one solid word. At best, we could translate groups of 2 or 3 numbers into words, but even that takes time. I think the fastest memorization times will be achieved when we can remember 851094 in one look or pass and just hold onto that meaningless garbage in our minds long enough to complete the solve. 8-5 goes diagonal on my cube, 10 is 2*5, walks to 9 on the bottom and then jumps diagonally to 4... 214. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Can you exercise memory?
From: JohnLouis Louis <pjlmem@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 11 Nov 2005 19:47:11 +0000 (GMT)

It is possible to memorise the cube in 30 sec. Bernett is doing it in 40 to 60 sec. There is no need to convert at the time of memorising because all the words for different numbers are fixed already. For example 914 is battery, 357 is milk 6,0 ,12 is chosen 11, 8, 2 is tiffin is etc. Once you have mastered the phonetic sound and value system and either learn to convert quickly on the spot or you have pre-determined word for every combination, you will be the fastest man again!!! As Stefan said try 4*4*4 and 5*5*5 blindfolded after meeting the cute girl PHYSICS GRE on saturday. You might understand the utility and validity of this system. Decode the > words into numbers when you are actually solving the cube. > Â¤ > John Louis > > mike_go_uk <no_reply@yahoogroups.com> wrote: >> --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, JohnLouis Louis >> <pjlmem@y...> wrote: >> > >> > Talc = 75 >> > gypsum = 6903 >> > feldspar = 851094 >> > topaz = 190 >> > corundum = 74213 >> > diamond = 1321. >> >Â¤ Â¤ Â¤ Â¤ Â¤ Â¤ using phonetic sound and value system, any number can be >> converted into any meaningful word or vice versa. >> >> But here you have some meaningful words already (at least, I already >> have a notion of the relative hardness of 5 out of the six substances >> named).Â¤ You've converted them into meaningless numbers: 74213 is no >> friend of mine. ;) >> >> Mike >> >> >> >> >> >> > Enjoy this Diwali with Y!
