322. Wow!!!! how to use commutators very consistently
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Mon, 05 Dec 2005 20:54:22 -0000

Hey Rob, you just gave me an idea for how to make my solving much more consistent and make my biggest problem about undoing setup moves literally not a problem at all. Say I have the three pieces UFl, URf, and DRf. If I have to cycle them counterclockwise I do: [R' d' R, U'] and if I had to cycle them clockwise I would do the inverse, or [U', R' d' R] This made it hard to remember if I started with a slice move, or the actual edge placing maneuver. However, using your idea for commutators I can always start with the edge placing maneuver, and always end with a slice move (saving almost all my trouble for remembering undoing setup moves). So counterclockwise is the same with [R' d' R, U'] and clockwise becomes [l D' l', U] which is of the same form of [place stuff , slice move, inverse of placing stuff, inverse slice move]. Wow, this is awesome! I think having all my commutators of the same form like this will greatly improve my accuracy, and hopefully also the speed. This is cool, thanks again! Chris --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > > I like to cycle my edges: > > > > UFl --- > ULf --- > URf --- > UFl > > > > using the following alg: > > > > F' dR'd'L dRd'L' F > >

Hi Chris, As usual you have taken some simple ideas and really run a long way with them. Some awesome ideas! IÂ¤ll answer all three posts at once Â¤ howÂ¤s that for efficiency ;) >>I guess I want to ask to make sure, but do you always use that same alg to move the edges, and use setup moves every time? No not quite always, but at least 90 percent if not 95 percent. Of course I also use the inverse if it makes set ups much easier. Occasionally I will also cycle edges in the Â¤bÂ¤ slice instead of the Â¤fÂ¤ slice but only if it makes both set ups much easier. For this I use a very similar alg but with u moves instead of d moves: F u L uÂ¤ RÂ¤ u LÂ¤ uÂ¤ R FÂ¤ (and its inverse) Also, if UFl ends up solved because I am faced with 2 or 3 shorter cycles instead of one nice long 23 cycle (my favourite scenario) than I use an adaptation of my usual alg designed to cycle out of UFr (if it is still unsolved). >>why don't you consider cycling as: d R' d' L d R d' L' and cycle ULf -> URf -> FLd Yeah, I did think about that. It would indeed save two moves every time I use it. But in the end I decided to stick with my original plan because I found that it made set up moves easier. I consider UFl to be my Â¤distribution centerÂ¤ if you will. I generally use L and R moves to place my two target pieces into UfL and UfR. If FLd were my Â¤distribution centerÂ¤ it would make the UfL set up move much more awkward. >>I have noticed that sometimes if I forget the setup move I did, I can think of the position the pieces started in and think how I would set them up. If unsure how to undo a set up move I will often ask myself how I would normally do it in the first place. This will refresh my memory and make the inverse much more obvious. Some of my proudest bld moments come not from perfect solves but realizing that I have made an error and being able to backtrack to fix it. >>However, using your idea for commutators ... Not really my idea. I improvised the alg by adapting one that Masayuki had on his site for LL solving 4x4x4 and 5x5x5 using his old technique. If I remember correctly his alg cycled 3 edges on the D face and I simply changed it to cycle three edges on the U face. He is an awesome cuber and a super nice guy so I think the credit for the idea should go to him :) >>I am finding, that what is helping me to both get faster and more accurate for 4x4 (I have yet to try 5x5, so this might not apply there) is to use commutators on all faces, from any angle. There are cases where I used to use a setup move that now I can see that I can use a commutator from a different angle and have no setup moves at all. At first I lost a lot of accuracy since I wasn't used to doing commutators like that, but now I am getting used to it and I think I want this to be my approach in the future. Awesome idea! I think this is really interesting and I will probably try this direct multi-angle approach tonight with a couple of eyes open solves. If I can really get a good feel for your new approach I might well convert to it Â¤ I think that it really has a lot of potential to be powerful and fast. But for me for now though I will stick to old faithful and his huge catalogue of set up moves. The multi-angle would save a lot of time and set ups but it adds another level of complexity to the technique. Always using the same 3 cycle with set ups is really simple, requires no additional thinking on the fly, and also adheres to the cardinal rule of consistency. I have also found that the more I do big bld solves the easier set up moves become. >> Best of luck with your awesome BLD achievements, I'm still jealous about the 5x5x5 heh heh :-P Now now now, havenÂ¤t you read anything that Stefan and Dr Carr post? 325. cyclic shifts
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 06 Dec 2005 01:36:34 -0000

Hey everyone, I got this idea from Per Fredlund, and have recently been using it for BLD 4x4x4. One example from my solve today was the following center cycle, dfL -> rdF -> blU Normally I would do the setup move r then [r' d r, U2] but since the setup move and the first move of my commutator are inverses of eachother, I've started doing the cyclic shift (perform the same algorithm, but start at a point beyond the first move, when you reach the end start over and do the beginning moves until you reach the move you started with). So in my solve I did (d r U2 r' d' r U2 r') starting at move 2 and wrapping around again to move 1. I know this probably sounds very trivial, but the thing is this was my very first time actually trying the cyclic shift technique for a cycle in a solve, and unfortunately I forgot to apply the r' at the end. This was at the end of my center cycle, and thus the end of my solve so it was a very, very close DNF. Anyway, again I know it is causing me DNFs in the shortrun, but I am trying to train myself to see these cyclic shifts also. I think having a run of DNFs for a while will be more than balanced out by having so much more freedom for performing my center cycles, and having to use less setup moves (my main goal for all stages of 4x4x4 BLD). I guess I am trying to optimize my approach for having no setup moves whatsoever (if that's even possible), but it is my goal. I don't know if cyclic shifting is something people already do but I wanted to mention it. I know in theory it is such a simple idea, but for BLD solving it needs to be practiced to become second nature as my DNF today shows :-( Anyway, I think with all of these optimizations that I can come that much closer to my final goal of a sub-10 minute solve. I hope this is what I need to get there, but I imagine that I'll discover other cool tricks along the way. So anyway, I am DNFing because of them now, but I will eventually be using these cyclic shifts in my solving. I just wanted to post in case anyone had not considered using these before. Chris

327. Re: cyclic shifts
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 06 Dec 2005 19:17:52 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > One example from my solve today was the following center cycle, > > dfL -> rdF -> blU > > Normally I would do the setup move r then [r' d r, U2] but since the > setup move and the first move of my commutator are inverses of > eachother, I've started doing the cyclic shift (perform the same > algorithm, but start at a point beyond the first move, when you > reach the end start over and do the beginning moves until you reach > the move you started with). > > So in my solve I did (d r U2 r' d' r U2 r') starting at move 2 and > wrapping around again to move 1. Hmm... how do you think about it as a cyclic shift? I think about this as an ordinary obvious commutator. First I see that Lfd and Frd are the the ones that are a single layer turn away from each other. So I want Ulb to replace one of them. Replacing Frd is easier, works with rU2r', suggesting the commutator [rU2r',d]. But since the cycle goes the other direction, I use the inverse [d,rU2r'], i.e. same you used. I just call this a "backwards" commutator and I call the [replace, singleturn] a "forwards" commutator. But I don't think about cyclic shifts for this. > I guess I am trying to optimize my approach for having no setup > moves whatsoever (if that's even possible), but it is my goal. How do you 3-cycle the outer edges URf->RFu->FUr->URf ? Cheers! Stefan

332. odd edge cycles
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 06 Dec 2005 19:54:52 -0000

Ok, so I've been thinking about this a lot and wanted to ask what people thought. I'm trying to think of ways to finish off an odd edge cycle for 4x4x4. What I've been doing lately is using this 4x4x4 supercube centers safe alg to swap just two edge pieces: r2 B2 U2 l U2 r' U2 r U2 F2 r F2 l' B2 r2 R L U2 L' R' U' R L U2 L' R' U' And I would use setup moves to get the edges next to eachother somewhere. However, you could swap two edge groups, and end two edge cycles, with: d R F' U R' F d' u' F' R U' F R' u This alg is also centers safe, but I imagine the setup moves to get the edges where you need them would be horrendous. Anyway, I don't really have any other ideas, I already save odd corner cycles for last, and that takes a good bit of thought effort to remember (especially with a fast centers solve!) so I think I don't want to save odd edge cycles till the end. Does anyone else have any ideas? How would you guys do the edges if there was an 8 cycle, two 2-cycles, and a 12 cycle? (I really had this once during a solve) Chris
333. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: cyclic shifts
From: Rob Butler <futuese@...>

Date: Tue, 6 Dec 2005 11:58:28 -0800 (PST)

--- Stefan Pochmann <pochmann@...> wrote: > > How do you 3-cycle the outer edges > URf->RFu->FUr->URf ? Without inventing a commutator on the fly I would just use a set up move and the UFr mirror of my regular 3-cycle: u2 L F d'LdR' d'L'dR F' L' u2 Though I suspect you were looking for something more direct. The thing is that I can 'see' these things very quickly and easily with my regular alg + inverse + mirrors and I can execute them with a high degree of accuracy. This one only took me about one second to figure out using my method. I tried last night but was admittedly no good at generating commutators on the fly - a success rate only somewhere around 50 percent and that was with my eyes open. I will definately keep trying though because I believe it would be a really good skill to acquire through perseverence and practice. I might even give it a shot wih my eyes closed because I find visualization a lot easier without all the other colours and pieces acting as a distraction. As for cyclic shifts I guess it could also be viewed as move cancellation. Personally I generally avoid cancelling moves while blindfolding. Saving 2 moves in the r slice is a very small profit for the risk of a DNF. By never cancelling moves it means I always use the same alg without fail. No thinking, no questions. If the first move of the alg cancells the last move of my set up then I just have a quick laugh at my own expense and keep on solving. Rob __________________________________________ Yahoo! 335. Re: odd edge cycles
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>

336. Big cubes - as easy as 3,4,5 !
From: "Rob Butler" <futuese@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Tue, 06 Dec 2005 22:15:30 -0000

Well that was my most ambitious attempt to date and also my most satisfactory DNF. Yes, I did try to solve a 3x3x3, a 4x4x4, AND a 5x5x5 in a row. Result - DNF The 3x3x3 was perfect The 5x5x5 was perfect but the 4x4x4 had 4 edges and 8 centers misplaced. This tells me that I must have done a r instead of an r' at some point in my solve (or vice versa). So close but literally missed by a single wrong turn! Since I set out to do all three I will NOT claim this as a 3x3x3 and a 5x5x5, just as a humble DNF. I started from smallest to largest so I am still kinda happy that I was able to keep my focus enough to do a 5x5x5 perfectly even after the 3x3x3 and the 4x4x4. I wanted to push my boundaries in terms of amount of data in memorization and found it to be no problem at all. I will definately have to try this again just to even the score. Total solve time was 53 minutes. I have no idea how long I spent doing the memorization. Rob

Actually, another question I have is the break down of the memorization vs. the execution time. For a 1:23 second solve, memorization must have been around 30 seconds... perhaps less? Execution in under 40 seconds is extremely rare and difficult. 341. Re: Big cubes - as easy as 3,4,5 !
From: "kyuubree" <agent_m80@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 09 Dec 2005 02:03:45 -0000

This is Marcus. Amazing, Rob! My only suggestion to you is to maximize your memorization abilities. I recommend memorizing things in chunks. If you are good at binary, you could use something like that for the orientation of edges. Here is one such method I use sometimes if the orientations of a 3x3x3 are a little messy (I usually use this during a relay and not a speedsolve, as relays are more memory intensive and therefore it is better to get a sure-fire method for long-term "data recovery," so to speak): Looking at the top layer of the cube, assign a value to edges 1, 2, 3, and 4 with values, 1, 2, 4, and 8, respectively. You simply add the numbers from the edges that need flipping. For example, the "flip number" for Layer 1, assuming that, say, Edges 1 and 3 are oriented incorrectly, would be 1 + 4 (since Edge 1's value is 1 and Edge 3's value is 4). Therefore the flip number is 5. Do this procedure for all three layers and you have three numbers, which can be associated with words depending on the first letter. For example, 5 could be Eat, or Erase, or Egg, whatever (since the fifth letter of the alphabet is 'e'). Example: You memorized the edges using the phrase "Foolish Honey Muffin." Alright, that's just gravy and all, but you know the letters are F, H, and M, which are 6, 8, and 13, respectively. 6 can only be broken down into 2 + 4 (this means Edges 2 and 3 on Layer 1 need flipping). 8 can only be broken down into 8 (this means Edge 3 on the middle layer needs flipping), and 13 can only be broken down into 1 + 4 + 8 (this means Edges 1, 3, and 4 need flipping). All with merely memorizing "Foolish Honey Muffin" :) Using tricks like these will ensure that you never forget what the cube layout looks like.

Thanks Marcus. That is a great tip! Actually what made me most nervous about the solve was the thought of doing the 4 and 5 perfect but missing the 3! Along the same lines I found memorizing the 5 long 24 cycles quite straightforward. Ironically what caused me worry was the relatively simple corner solves - the 3 corner orientations and 3 corner permutations. I was worried that I might forget such a simple but short sequence simply because it is so short and bland - they just don't stand out as much! The long sequences are memorized by a funny story so they take longer to compose but never fail to come to mind during the solve. I agree with you in that I like to memorize things in chunks. I solve using a cycle method. For the 24 cycles I much prefer one long sequence to 2,3,4, or even 5 shorter sequences. Assuming I have a nice long sequence I like to memorize in pairs since that is the number of edges/centers I will solve with any one alg. The pairs are memorized by a single word/concept. Then I build my stories around pairs of words (i.e. units which represent 4 edges). Of course pairs of words can be combined along a theme for 8 edges - and so on, and so on, and so on. I was a system heavily based on the major system but with a couple of little personal tweeks to made it more directly applicble to cubing. Your technique for orientation will be really useful for these bigger attempts. I will definately try again after I take a week's break (or more with the chaos of Christmas season - clearly I prefer more sedate times when I KNOW I will be left alone for such attempts) Maybe this tip will be of some use to you or someone else: For corner permutation on simple 3x3x3 solves I just memorize the numbers - so few and so easy for short term with little data. On bigger cubes and my 3,4,5 attempt I also used number affiliation. Again I memorized in pairs but I tried affiliating the numbers with hockey players. Eg: 2,4,6,8,1,3,7 Would be (Brian McCabe 24), (Jaromir Jagr 68), (Mats Sundin 13), (Gary Roberts 7). Choose numbers according to your favourite sports/teams/players. I live in Toronto so I usually choose Maple Leafs :) Rob --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "kyuubree" <agent_m80@h...> wrote: > > This is Marcus. > > > Amazing, Rob! 344. Re: Big cubes - as easy as 3,4,5 !
From: "kyuubree" <agent_m80@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 09 Dec 2005 03:10:07 -0000

Excellent advice. The more unusual the memory method, the better it works (especially stories). As long as you always associate things in a different way each time (for example, if you associate CAR with a certain piece, don't always make the story involving the car driving somewhere. Have new stuff happen to it each time to keep the memory hooks more profoud. Have it get stolen by something, or have it hit a cat, or whatever!). But yeah a 3x3x3 can be broken down into a few simple phrases, really. You could memorize the phrases, come back three years later, and still be able to solve it. The key is to make something too funny to forget (at least, that's what works for me. I always make things as absurd as possible, like if I have E H G for example, I'll think Electric Horse Grease or something. It's weird enough to never leave the memory). Such methods work well for relays. After that you learn to memorize more quickly, and as you learn more algorithms, it all starts to come together. --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "Rob Butler" <futuese@y...> wrote: > > > Thanks Marcus. > > That is a great tip! 346. Re: Big cubes - as easy as 3,4,5 !
From: "kyuubree" <agent_m80@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 09 Dec 2005 06:08:56 -0000

Chris, With edge pieces for virtually any cube, including the 4x4x4, I use the story method, but in a visual fashion. For example, the upper- front-left wing might be an old man's house, and let's say the next cube in the cycle happened to be the lower-front-left wing. I would imagine a waterfall going literally down the cube from wing to wing. And then let's say the next edgewing of the cycle was the one between the B and R faces, on the lower half of the cube. I would imagine there being a newly-built rollercoaster at the bottom of that waterfall, spiraling through the water and curving around to hit the opposite side, but since it's a new rollercoaster, it doesn't quite make it to the upper edge, but rather the lower one (which you want). I am sorry if that didn't make any sense. The basic idea is to create a visual story on the cube itself for cubes with multiple edges and wings. For example, a permutation of edges might make the shape of a bow or cross if you were to draw a line from edge to edge within the same cycle. In short, visual on-cube physical patterns are effective ways to memorize edge cycles quickly. The trick is to be able to figure out perverted/weird/disgusting/cool stories on the spot so you will remember your memory hooks, as you have already eloquently stated. As for how long memorization takes, I really don't know. I'd say a 7-minute memorization time for the 4x4x4 is a very good time. The corners are the same as a 3x3x3 so those should take the same amount of time, obviously, as a 3x3x3. Then stories for the edges are good. For the 4x4x4 method I made up a binary method similar to the 3x3x3 edge orientation method but I haven't actually used it in a while. As for solving the 4x4x4 straight-up blindfolded, I just never really got around to it, I guess? I was really thirsting after the 5x5x5, mainly because it's an odd (as opposed to even) cube. Even cubes have a certain life of their own. Anyways I hope this helped. There are other methods to doing these things that I would be glad to share if you find that the methods stated above are inadequate or insufficient. --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > Hey Marcus, > > Yes I also try to makes stories funny or absurd too. 349. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Nature or Nurture?
From: Tyson Mao <tmao@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 9 Dec 2005 14:42:00 -0800

In physical sports such as running, you can train very hard but ultimately, nature takes over in the levels of the elite. It doesn't matter how hard you train, someone else will do the same, and they'll be better with you. For memory with blindfold cubing, I have a feeling that it works oppositely. Someone who's very gifted at memory might have very fast improvement to begin with. Someone who isn't gifted at memory will have a more difficult time, but ultimately with training, I think both minds can reach the same level. When I started out, I was taking maybe 3 minutes to memorize the cube. I had very rapid improvement and can do under 60 seconds almost every time with good times being between 40 and 50. Leyan, on the other hand, claims his memory is bad, but I've seen him do some pretty good stuff and I'm sure he's been working on it quite a bit. I don't really know. Most of this is all theory of course. I do know that 15-25 seconds memorization is quite insane, and I'd be more than happy with 30 :-P. 350. Re: Nature or Nurture?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 10 Dec 2005 00:01:31 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > Can I also get to 30-40 second 3x3 memorization times, or does my > brain just have to be wired differently than it is now to do it? I'm sure you can. I think it's similar to running 200 meters. Can you run those in 30-40 seconds? Sure. Pretty much everybody can, or can train enough to be able to. Can you run them in 20 seconds? Probably not. Whether you can run in 30-40 seconds or not depends not on your abilities, but on your will to do it and maybe your will to work for it. For 20 seconds it's different, most people will never be able to do that no matter how hard they work. What I'm trying to say is that the "ability" issue is a small and very low area. Same for memorizing the 3x3 in 30-40 seconds, I believe that's something pretty much everybody can learn with enough work. Ability will distinguish further below, maybe also around 20 seconds. I don't think we've reached already what's really possible. After all, how many people have really put a lot of effort into this? Maybe in 2 years people will think you're slow if you take more than 40 seconds. Cheers! Stefan

352. Re: [blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube] Re: Nature or Nurture?
From: Sachin Shirwalkar <sachinss@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 10 Dec 2005 12:43:38 +0530

Lets be hopeful that it dosent depend on nature alone or else i would be doomed! You see i have a very bad memory to start with so just memorising the cube was a big task for me at the start. But i had read in some memory improvement book that the power of self suggestion has amazing effects.So i tired that on myself, telling me that even though my long term memory is bad, my short term is good, I kept repeating that to myself and sure enough i dropped my memorisation times from about ~5 mins to about ~ 2.5 mins now. This efect may just be because of practice or maybe psychological...i cannot tell. But i have seen improvememnts in areas unrelated to the cube also, so maybe this could be due to the self suggestion thing. I know that i still have a very long way to go, but seeing from where i have started, im quite satisfied with me. Sachin. To the 4x4 blindsolvers: How long do the 4x4 edges take you *not* blindfolded but with your blindsolving method? I've done this a few times, *not* blindfolded: scramble and solve centers+corners normally (i.e. speedsolving), then take the time for solving edges with my blindfold method but not blindfolded. I'm usually around 3-4 minutes, how good is that? Cheers! Stefan

Rather than slowly climbing over the wall, I run against it fast and often until it breaks down :-) Cheers! Stefan

372. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 14 Dec 2005 17:11:00 -0000

Wow Stefan that video was very impressive! It looks like you are using a RrUu alg, or maybe more than one, to solve the edges. After watching that I think you would find my edge video quite boring. Basically when presented with the next three pieces to cycle I look for the best base face, my commutators are of the form [alg to place an edge, turn the base slice/face]. So I'll spend a few seconds figuring out the best setup moves (2 is too many, but sometimes I'm forced to use 2, I try for 1 or to find a commutator based on how they're setup already). Then I do an 8 move commutator at roughly the speed you execute your alg, then I undo the setup moves slowly and move to the next three. I can't make a video until I get home, so maybe 4-5 days from now, but after watching yours mine will appear quite boring to watch. Chris --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > I just made a video where I got 2:03.03: > http://stefan-pochmann.de/foo/4x4_bld_edges_20303.wmv > (4978 kb) > > Maybe I'm now twisting about as fast as Dror does, watching him solve > 4x4 blindfolded is spectacular. 373. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 14 Dec 2005 20:03:43 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, Pedro <pedrosino1@y...> wrote: > > WOW! I'm simply amazed...how can you do the centers soooooooooo fast? Well, I never played with a real 4x4x4, but on gabbasoft's software I can't do 1 center in 10 seconds...doing all of them...pff.. .no chance... One trick is ... sshhh ... don't tell anyone ... one trick is ... solve only five sides and you get the sixth for free :-). That's actually really part of my method. Alright here's my average for edges: 2:11.49, 2:08.62, 2:22.31, (1:36.52), 2:41.81, 2:25.22, 2:14.40, 2:25.49, 2:15.28, (3:02.38), 2:59.92, 1:56.67 = 2:22.12 This average alone convinced me that part of my method has to go. 377. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Wed, 14 Dec 2005 23:17:15 -0000

Wow this is really really really easy!!! I can just think of the two transpositions like this, I have (AB) and (CD). So I cycle A to B where it's supposed to go, and B to either spot in the other transposition. After that the A spot (which started as my main spot) then cycles to the other spot in the other transposition (the one I didn't cycle to) which then cycles to the first spot in the second transposition. That sounds really hard to say in words, but on the cube it is such an easy quick little maneuver!! Stefan, let me practice this and really get it down, then I'll take another average. Doing my solves this way will mean that I only 1/2 the time have to do my super ugly 27 move alg, and then only once per solve. And also I can keep my same solving order, which I have really grown to like since it makes my memorization the most comfortable (I would argue memorization for bigger cubes BLD is the MOST important part of the whole solve). Heh heh wow this is cool. Give me some time to get used to doing this, and I'll post another average. Tee hee, man I love BLD solving, it's so much fun :-D Chris --- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > Hmm.. > > I've thought about solving by pairs of transpositions for ending off > more than one odd edge cycle. > > So if I have two odd cycles and I work them down to say (AB) and (CD) > to solve the cube. > > Performing (ABC) leaving me with (ADC) and that solves the cube in two > 8 move commutators. > > So I can solve with two commutators rather than 54 moves of supercube > safe parity fixing nonsense. > > Hmmmm... gotta start messing around with this. My question to you big cube BLD'ers is this. How should I solve in competition? I mean I want to get a good time of course, but it's more important to me to just get a successful solve. I've found that what allows me to remember the edge and center cycles is to just memorize superfast, so that by the time I actually start solving them, not much time has passed since I finished memorizing them. I definitely go for the recency effect here and just try to rehearse the edge cycles (my middle memorization step) a bit more than centers so that I don't get interference from centers or corners. Anyway, I was just curious how I should train for a competition? Should I solve a bit slower and memorize better, or should I continue memorizing quickly and taking advantage of having centers and edges very very recently in my short term memory so that I don't forget them? 382. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 15 Dec 2005 22:35:51 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > Alright here's my average for edges: > > 2:11.49, 2:08.62, 2:22.31, (1:36.52), 2:41.81, 2:25.22, 2:14.40, > 2:25.49, 2:15.28, (3:02.38), 2:59.92, 1:56.67 = 2:22.12 Wow, that's really good! Quite inconsistent, but I guess that will go once you treat odd cycles better. > I'd say I average 1 setup move overall. Sometimes I can see a > commutator with the way they're setup, and equally as often I need 2 > setup moves. Most of the time I do 1 setup move. So I'd say I > average right at 1 setup move overall. Wow, I have to try it again then. I think I did and it took me at least 2 most of the time. Question: do you have a certain edge place that is always part of every 3-cycle you do? Or do you do completely free 3-cycles? Ok, now here's my method: First scramble the cube and then solve centers and corners speedstyle. Now we can begin. First please also solve the URf edge any way you like. It's not necessary to do this at the start but it will make the explanation much easier. Do you know my 3x3 blindsolving method and how I solve the edges? I have a fixed buffer position from which I solve the edges one by one. That fixed buffer position is UR. For my 4x4 edges, I use the same idea: Solve the edges one by one, shooting from a certain buffer. But this time, the buffer is not fixed at one position: It alternates between UBr and URf. At the start (after you solved the URf edge) the buffer is UBr. So look at the edge in that position and bring its goal place (i.e. the edge currently in that place) to FUr. Now do this algorithm: R U R' U' (Rr) R' U R U' (Rr)' This brings the edge from UBr to FUr so now undo your setup moves and the edge will be solved. Now the buffer is at URf. So look at the edge in that position and bring its goal place (i.e. the edge currently in that place) to FUr. Now do the inverse of the above algorithm, i.e.: (Rr) U R' U' (Rr)' R U R U' R' This brings the edge from URf to FUr so now undo your setup moves and the edge will be solved. Now the buffer is at UBr again. So repeat from now on. Is that clear enough to understand it? I pretend to use 2-edge-swaps by alternating between two buffer positions. So I think of it as solving with swaps, solving one edge at a time, exactly like my 3x3 method. Cheers! Stefan
383. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Thu, 15 Dec 2005 23:20:33 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@g...> wrote: > > Is that clear enough to understand it? I pretend to use 2-edge-swaps > by alternating between two buffer positions. So I think of it as > solving with swaps, solving one edge at a time, exactly like my 3x3 > method. Uh, just to add the benefits: - Always setting up only one edge is easy for the brain. - There are only 22 possible targets so you can *know* setup moves for all of them, and all 16 under the U layer are even covered by two very simple rules. - Use only two algs and they are very fast and easy (actually you might use them already for 3x3 OLL, at least I do). Cheers! Stefan
384. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>

Hey Stefan, Yeah that's really cool. And I can see how that creates parity and you will have to either "solve parity" or not. What do you do if both buffer spots solve though? That is something that also gives me problems with your method for 3x3x3. I just don't know how to handle if the buffer spot solves. For my method I always start with UBl. That is my starting spot. If that spot is solved I start at UBr. If that spot is also solved I move around the U layer to find an unsolved edge, etc.. Ok, so I see where UBl goes, then where the piece in that spot goes too. Then I just solve via 3 cycles same as regular 3x3 cycles BLD. If the cycle involving UBl is shorter than a 24 cycle, then I find the other cycle(s) by hunting for pieces that weren't in my first cycle. Once I have all 24 pieces accounted for I solve all the even cycles, and all the odd cycles down to just 2 pieces swapped. Then I used to use my ugly 27 move alg for all odd cycles, but now I use the 2 three cycles idea (which I missed from your earlier post somehow) and if I am left finally with only two edges swapped then i use my 27 move alg. Also do you solve centers before edges? I can see how that would make your choice of algs much more varied. I have to do everything "supercube safe" since my centers are still unsolved when I am solving edges. I was wondering why you said to solve centers before edges for our examples, since I would only have corners done (or simplified to a two swap) at this point. :-D I definitely like your method, but I am partial to the freestyle method personally. I am horrible at remembering setup moves (I am for 3x3x3 too). I think using the freestyle approach helps me overcome this, I would definitely have trouble with remembering the setup moves if I used your approach. I think the number of moves is not much of an issue for your method, your thinking process is MUCH faster than mine, so the more moves are executed much faster. I like my approach since it lets me overcome my weak spot of dealing with setup moves, but I can see how your method can be amazingly fast. 386. Re: getting more fluid (catching old mistakes)
From: "cmhardw" <foozman17@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 00:38:55 -0000

> What are the primary algorithms that you use to solve the 4x4x4 BLD, > by the way? I can post some of mine later on tonight as I have to > run off to a final, but my execution time for the 4x4x4 is a bit > slower and I am wondering if it is due to longer algs (and HELLA > slower than you and Stefan turning-wise... holy crap guys). Hey Marcus, I really don't use algorithms in the sense that I think you mean. I just use types of commutators, but I perform them from any angle and on any face. I am usually cycles from the lUB piece, but if that piece solves I will still do commutators on other faces. Here is an exaple of all the types of commutators I use involving lUB. lUB -> rUF -> dFL [B d' B, U2] and also the inverse for the opposite direction cycle lUB -> rUF -> uFL [B' u2 B, U2] lUB -> fUL -> dFL [B d' B', U] lUB -> fUL -> uFL [B' u2 B, U] lUB -> dFL -> fUL U B d' B' U B d B' U2 lUB -> lUF -> fUL [l, F' L F] lUB -> lUF -> bUL [B L' B', l'] lUB -> dFL -> dBR [L' U' L, d2] The list goes on really. I also recognize lots of positions that are 1 move away from one of those positions. I really tried to turn the process of coming up with a commutator into one that is totally intuitive. I really do just come up with these on the fly, but I would say that the ones above are the typical forms that I use, but I don't just do them that way. I would also do the reflections, rotations to other faces, etc.. I do like the commutators of the form where I do 3 outer face turns, and the single slice is an inner slice. They are much faster to do, since I don't have to do double layer turns. For the centers I use mostly the same ideas, I try to keep them of the same form, or same rough idea, just for centers instead of edges. I mean I hope this helps? I consider my edges and centers solving to be 100% intuitive, since I understand exactly how each commutator works, and I just try to find the shortest one (with no setup moves, or as few as possible) to solve the three pieces next in my cycle. Hope that helps? Chris
387. Re: 4x4 edges - time ?
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Fri, 16 Dec 2005 00:48:57 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "cmhardw" <foozman17@h...> wrote: > > What do you do if both buffer spots solve though? That is something > that also gives me problems with your method for 3x3x3. I just don't > know how to handle if the buffer spot solves. Actually I don't like to think of it as two buffers. Only as one. It's switching between two places, but at one point of time, it's only at one of the two places. The "buffer edge", i.e. the edge that I ignore the whole time and only solve the other 23, is the UBr edge. The URf edge is also special, it's a "helper" for the buffer. It should only move back and forth between the two possible buffer places as an unwanted side effect, similar to the URF and UBR corners swapping back and forth as a side effect when I solve 3x3 edges. Ideally this URf edge is already solved at the start, but since this is usually not the case, I try to let it "slip in" when it appears in a cycle. This is the part of my method I haven't yet fully thought through, though (what a great sentence ending :-). To better understand my 4x4 edges method, I'd recommend you first understand my 3x3 method if you have problems with that. If the buffer edge is already solved but other pieces are unsolved, then I simply "break into" another cycle by pulling one of the bad pieces out of the cycle and into the buffer. Then I solve that cycle as usual. > Also do you solve centers before edges? Yes I do. But the main reason I suggested you solve centers+corners before doing edges blindfold-style, is that you can take centers+corners as a reference then. If you screw something up, you will notice because of destroyed centers or corners. And at the end, the cube will be solved so that's more beautiful. And solving centers takes me less time than solving corners anyway :-) > I would definitely have trouble with remembering the setup moves > if I used your approach. What?!? I don't think so. There are *overall* only 22 setup algs that you'll *ever* need with my method. And they're obvious. I see green/white, I setup with D F2. You also practice all of them with every solve! The small number of possibilities allows you to *know* a setup alg for *every* possibility. You don't make them up on the fly. Instead, they are hard-coded in your brain. You don't need to remember the setup alg. You only need to remember the edge you just solved, and then undo its setup alg. But I'll try your approach, too, and see whether my brain can do that :-) Cheers! Stefan

401. Re: Got a 4x4x4! Wanna blindsolve it now :D
From: "Stefan Pochmann" <pochmann@...>
To: blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com
Date: Sat, 31 Dec 2005 01:44:33 -0000

--- In blindfoldsolving-rubiks-cube@yahoogroups.com, "thomkirjava" <snkenjoi@g...> wrote: > > I'm using L' F' (l' U2) (l' U2) x (U2 l') (U2 r) x' (U2 r') (U2 l2) F > L for now, but there's probibly better. Yeah, for example you could remove the L' and L :-) > Just one last question: how does the helper buffer edge get shoved in? > The only way I can think of is to look for it at the start and > consider it to act as the piece in the helper buffer position. The, > when you execute, you swap these two pieces either at the start or end > of the solve. I don't get the whole 'slip-it-in' idea yet. > > Aside from that, it's a nice method :). Thanks. That slip-in is a detail I haven't thought through yet. You can of course solve that buffer helper right away before doing anything else. At least for me it makes thinking easier (and it made the general explanation easier). Ok, let me think about slip-in, i.e. solving it on the fly. The idea is that this helper (URf) would ideally be solved (i.e. URf piece being in URf place) and just switch back and forth between URf and UBr. And the other of the two positions would be the buffer from which I shoot an edge to the correct position. So let's imagine we come across that helper piece *during* the solve, i.e. it lands in the buffer. Let's first consider what I achieve in each step: 1 The piece currently in the buffer ends up solved 2 The piece previously in the position of the now solved piece is now in the buffer 3 The buffer has switched between UBr and URf Now how do we react if we find the helper in the buffer? This means the bufferPlace contains the helperPiece. The answer: Do nothing! Just pretend you've done a step and continue. Why is this correct? 3 bufferPlace has switched from UBr to URf (by pretending so) 1 helperPiece got solved (it's at UBr, the other of the two places) 2 The piece which was in helperPlace is now in the buffer That surprises me, I'm somewhat shocked it's so easy. Man, I should've thought about this earlier... Cheers! Stefan

Ooops. The setup moves for that alg are suppost to be R'F' and FR. The helper edge method you have is pretty cool, Took me a while but eventually I got my head around how it works. I memorise spatially, so I'll just think that piece is 'hot' or something. Another idea I had this morning, was to just find the helper piece and turn the cube around so it is in place. Just pretend you've done a step and continue. > Why is this correct? > > 3 bufferPlace has switched from UBr to URf (by pretending so) > 1 helperPiece got solved (it's at UBr, the other of the two places) > 2 The piece which was in helperPlace is now in the buffer > > That surprises me, I'm somewhat shocked it's so easy.
